14/10/2024, 20:10 - Vihaan: Bot Design:
Bot will be in humanoid form with 2 legs, head ( which contains sensors), foldable tray attached in belly area, and 2 gripper arms. Chest area will have a touch screen for interacting and taking input from customers.
Legs:
To save on cost legs will have limited range of motion just enough to climb stairs (hinges on  knees, hip and ankle region). Navigation on a single floor will be done through wheels. Each foot will have 4 wheels attached(sort of like skates) which will be retractable (when climbing stairs). This is done to speed up on floor travelling time and increase efficiency. 
Head:
Will have a combination of camera as well as ultrasonic sensors. Camera to detect objects, people, tables, obstacles. Ultrasonic sound sensor is for measuring distance between objects so as to adjust its speed. LiDAR is not used as it performs almost the same function as Ultrasonic and is more expensive. Using computer vision, it will detect obstacles and send signal to legs’ motors to avoid them.
Tray: To keep food, plates,menu etc. Foldable so that when not carrying food it is easier to bypass obstacles.
Screen: Will be the main source of input. When a customer wishes to order, the bot will display a unique QR code for that particular table number which customers can scan using mobile. This is done to increase efficiency. The bot can move way and do the other work while they order. The order through the webportal will be sent to the kitchen online.
However if such a websystem cannot be maintained or the user is unable to use the QR, menu items will be displayed on screen and users will tap to order items. 










Planning and communication between bots:

Assuming there are 4 floors and kitchen is on ground floor. So 1 bot is on each floor.
There is a centralised queue of operations on a server and each bot has its own individual queue. 
Operations examples: New customer entered, so get them menu. Food prepared so bring food. Take order from customer. Take payment. New plates.
Each operation will be assigned to a unique tablenum on a floor.
The server will assign these tasks according to the floor number to individual queues of the bots.
Each bot’s queue will have a  priority slot which will be executed soon after current job is finished. This is used for actions which affect other floor’s efficiency - as will be shown later. 
After every 3 tasks in the queue executed by the bot, it performs a scan of the floor: which is it checks if anyone on the floor has their hand raised, so it attends to them. Bot would display options on its screen like: 
Show Order QR
Change plates, get cutlery
Show Payment QR
Call manager

Users will interact with these through touch screen.
 For below example consider bot 1 on floor 1 and bot 2 on floor 2.
For bringing food from kitchen to a table following process is used(for example table on second floor):
When food is prepared, task “Deliver dish X to table Y in floor 2” is pushed into queue of Server. To execute this task server pushes “go to kitchen, get food and wait near staircase and give to bot2” to bot1’s priority slot. At the same time another task is pushed to bot2’s priority slot: “Go down staircase and collect food from bot1 and climb up stairs to deliver to required table”. This way the bot’s don’t wait for each other for long time due to the concept of priority slots. After this they continue to perform the operations of their respective stacks.
Actions such as showing payment/order QR, changing plates is added only 



to the local bot’s queue and not the server queue as they can be done independently. However stuff like when order needs to be delivered from one floor to another and requires inter-bot cooperation, it is pushed in server queue.

16/10/2024, 11:10 - Parth IITB: *a)	To handle the steps of size 30 cm the height of 4 of its legs are around 40-50cm.Legs are 4 to provide stability to the bot. Mostly(Robotic arm and legs) the material of bot is aluminium so that centre of gravity is lower for stability purposes.The box(to collect waste) attached to 4 legs can be of other lighter materials like plastic.The foot of the bot can be like 20cm.The legs of bot will be flexible with hinges at knees , hips and ankles.
b)	A light detector so that we know when it's night(evening or not) and a torch to deal with that , Computer vision so that it can detect waste.
c)	I have chosen robotic arm over something like vacuum cleaner type design because due to fluctuating water level bot might pick up some unnecessary water.Aluminium is water resistant so rain is not a problem (it forms unreactive aluminium oxide layer)The hole only opens when robotic arm is activated so that rain doesn’t spoil the day. To deal with heat aluminium is nice; its Melting point is 660 degree celsius (approx). For dealing with low light there is a light detector and torch. Also aluminium is cost effective
d)	Navigation will be ensured by computer vision. It will detect where the waste is, then the robotic arm picks it up and puts it into the box.It will also avoid the obstacles using the vision.There will be a certain code that if battery percentage is less than 10% it goes to a particular place which is fed into it and get charged and deposit the waste into a bigger dustbin there.
e)	First we turn the robot on.Robot scans the area. Wherever the garbage is nearest to it it goes to that location , uses its robotic hand picks that up and then repeats the process from that particular location.If the box is full then it returns to base location or if battery is less than 10% then also it returns to base location.
16/10/2024, 11:10 - Parth IITB: Weighing 1:
 Weigh B1 B2 B3 against B4 B5 B6 and weigh B7 B8 B9 against B10 B11 B12.
Case 1:
If the weighing scale of both of them are unbalanced then both of them have one contaminated block. Apply the algorithm given in part 1 to both of them.
Case 2:
If only one of them is unbalanced then without loss of generality let it be B1 B2 B3 B4 B5 B6 stack.
Weighing 2:
Weigh B1 B2 against B4 B5 and B3 against B6
Case 1:
B3 and B6 have equal weight then both the contaminated blocks are among B1 B2 B4 and B5.
Case 2:
If B3 and B6 do not weigh equal then one of them is contaminated and one of B1 B2 B4 and B5 is contaminated.
In this scenario we first find out which block is contaminated among B1 B2 B4 and B5 by the algorithm given in Part 1 Weighing 2 Case 2.
After that we weigh B3 with a non contaminated block if they are equal then the contaminated block is B6 else it's B3.

16/10/2024, 11:39 - Parth IITB: Part 1 
Your task is to devise an efficient algorithm for the MinerBot to identify the one block that is either lighter or heavier than the others using the fewest number of weighings. The correct identification of the faulty block is critical for maintaining the quality and reliability of your supply.
Solution:
One of the contaminated blocks is heavier and the other is lighter.
Weighing 1:
Weigh B1 B2 B3 against B4 B5 B6
If they are equal then the faulty block is among B7 , B8 … , B12
Without loss of generality let the faulty block be in B1 , B2 . .. … , B6 stack.

Weighing 2:
Weigh B1 B2 against B4 B5
Case 1:
If they are equal then the faulty block is either B3 or B6 weigh B1 and B3 if they are equal then B6 is faulty else B3 is faulty.
Case 2:
If the weighing scale is unbalanced let’s say towards B1 and B2 weigh B1 with B4.
Weighing 3:
Case 1: 
If B1 and B4 are equal then the faulty block is among B2 and B5 weigh B2 with B1 if they are equal then B5 is faulty else B2 is faulty.
Case 2:
If B1 and B4 are not equal then the faulty block is among them then just weigh B1 with B2 if they are equal the faulty block is B4 else the faulty block is B1.
Part 2 
You have received intel that the contamination can affect at most 2 blocks (but both are not of same weight) in any payload. How Would you modify the algorithm such that you can effectively identify all the contaminated samples in the minimum possible comparisons?

Solution:


Weighing 1:
 Weigh B1 B2 B3 against B4 B5 B6 and weigh B7 B8 B9 against B10 B11 B12.
Case 1:
If the weighing scale of both of them are unbalanced then both of them have one contaminated block. Apply the algorithm given in part 1 to both of them.
Case 2:
If only one of them is unbalanced then without loss of generality let it be B1 B2 B3 B4 B5 B6 stack.
Weighing 2:
Weigh B1 B2 against B4 B5 and B3 against B6
Case 1:
B3 and B6 have equal weight then both the contaminated blocks are among B1 B2 B4 and B5.
Case 2:
If B3 and B6 do not weigh equal then one of them is contaminated and one of B1 B2 B4 and B5 is contaminated.
In this scenario we first find out which block is contaminated among B1 B2 B4 and B5 by the algorithm given in Part 1 Weighing 2 Case 2.
After that we weigh B3 with a non contaminated block if they are equal then the contaminated block is B6 else it's B3.

20/10/2024, 23:33 - Vihaan: #include <iostream>
#include <string>

using namespace std;

// Structure to represent a Train Bogie
struct TrainBogie
{
    string name;      // Name of the bogie
    TrainBogie *next; // Pointer to the next bogie
};

// Function prototypes
TrainBogie *createBogie(const string &bogieName);
TrainBogie *insertAtBeginning(TrainBogie *head, const string &bogieName);
TrainBogie *insertAtEnd(TrainBogie *head, const string &bogieName);
TrainBogie *deleteAtBeginning(TrainBogie *head);
TrainBogie *deleteAtEnd(TrainBogie *head);
void searchAndAddAfter(TrainBogie *head, const string &existingBogieName, const string &newBogieName);
void searchAndDelete(TrainBogie *head, const string &bogieName);
void printList(TrainBogie *head);
TrainBogie *deleteEntireList(TrainBogie *head);

int main()
{
    int n;
    cin >> n;                   // Number of operations to perform
    TrainBogie *head = nullptr; // Initialize head pointer to null

    for (int i = 0; i < n; ++i)
    {
        int operation;
        string bogieName1, bogieName2;
        cin >> operation; // Read operation type

        // Perform operations based on user input
        if (operation == 1)
        { // Insert at the Beginning
            cin >> bogieName1;
            head = insertAtBeginning(head, bogieName1);
        }
        else if (operation == 2)
        { // Insert at the End
            cin >> bogieName1;
            head = insertAtEnd(head, bogieName1);
        }
        else if (operation == 3)
        { // Delete at the Beginning
            head = deleteAtBeginning(head);
        }
        else if (operation == 4)
        { // Delete at the End
            head = deleteAtEnd(head);
        }
        else if (operation == 5)
        { // Search and Add After
            cin >> bogieName1 >> bogieName2;
            searchAndAddAfter(head, bogieName1, bogieName2);
        }
        else if (operation == 6)
        { // Search and Delete
            cin >> bogieName1;
            searchAndDelete(head, bogieName1);
        }
        else if (operation == 7)
        { // Print the Entire List
            printList(head);
        }
        else if (operation == 8)
        { // Delete the Entire List
            head = deleteEntireList(head);
        }
    }

    return 0; // End of program
}

// Function to create a new TrainBogie node
TrainBogie *createBogie(const string &bogieName)
{
    TrainBogie *newBogie = new TrainBogie; // Allocate memory for new bogie
    newBogie->name = bogieName;            // Set the bogie name
    newBogie->next = nullptr;              // Initialize next to null
    return newBogie;                       // Return the new bogie
}

// Function to insert a bogie at the beginning of the list
TrainBogie *insertAtBeginning(TrainBogie *head, const string &bogieName)
{
    TrainBogie *newBogie = createBogie(bogieName); // Create a new bogie
    newBogie->next = head;                         // Point new bogie's next to current head
    return newBogie;                               // Return new head
}

// Function to insert a bogie at the end of the list
TrainBogie *insertAtEnd(TrainBogie *head, const string &bogieName)
{
    TrainBogie *newBogie = createBogie(bogieName);
    if (!head){
        
        head=newBogie;
        return head;
    }
    TrainBogie *temp = head; // Temporary pointer to traverse the list
    while (temp->next!=nullptr)
    { // Traverse to the last bogie
        temp = temp->next;
    }
    temp->next = newBogie;
    
    return head;
}

// Function to delete a bogie at the beginning of the list
TrainBogie *deleteAtBeginning(TrainBogie *head)
{
    TrainBogie *first=head;
    if (!head) return nullptr;
    head=head->next;
    delete first;
    return head;
}

// Function to delete a bogie at the end of the list
TrainBogie *deleteAtEnd(TrainBogie *head)
{
    if (!head)
        return nullptr; // If the list is empty, return null
    if (!head->next)
    {                   // If there's only one bogie
        delete head;    // Delete the head
        return nullptr; // List is now empty
    }
    TrainBogie *temp = head; // Temporary pointer to traverse the list
    while (temp->next->next)
    { // Traverse to the second-to-last bogie
        temp = temp->next;
    }
    delete temp->next;    // Delete the last bogie
    temp->next = nullptr; // Set the next of second-to-last bogie to null
    return head;          // Return original head
}

// Function to search for a bogie and add a new bogie after it
void searchAndAddAfter(TrainBogie *head, const string &existingBogieName, const string &newBogieName)
{
    TrainBogie *newBogie = createBogie(newBogieName);
    TrainBogie *temp = head; // Temporary pointer to traverse the list
    while (temp)
    { // Traverse the list
        if (temp->name == existingBogieName)
        {                                      // If the bogie to delete is found
            TrainBogie *nextSec = temp->next; // Store the bogie to delete
            newBogie->next=nextSec;     // Link previous bogie to next bogie
            temp->next=newBogie;
                              // Delete the bogie
            return;                            // Exit after deletion
        }
        temp = temp->next; // Move to the next bogie
        
    }
    return;

}

// Function to search for a bogie and delete it
void searchAndDelete(TrainBogie *head, const string &bogieName)
{
    if (!head)
        return; // If the list is empty, exit

    if (head->name == bogieName)
    {                                   // If the head bogie matches
        head = deleteAtBeginning(head); // Delete the head and update it
        return;                         // Exit after deletion
    }

    TrainBogie *temp = head; // Temporary pointer to traverse the list
    while (temp->next)
    { // Traverse the list
        if (temp->next->name == bogieName)
        {                                      // If the bogie to delete is found
            TrainBogie *toDelete = temp->next; // Store the bogie to delete
            temp->next = temp->next->next;     // Link previous bogie to next bogie
            delete toDelete;                   // Delete the bogie
            return;                            // Exit after deletion
        }
        temp = temp->next; // Move to the next bogie
    }
}

// Function to print the entire list
void printList(TrainBogie *head)
{
    if (!head)
    {
        cout << "-1" << endl; // Print -1 if the list is empty
        return;
    }
    TrainBogie *temp = head; // Temporary pointer to traverse the list
    while (temp)
    {                       // While there are bogies in the list
        cout << temp->name; // Print the bogie name
        if (temp->next)
        {
            cout << " > "; // Print separator if not the last bogie
        }
        temp = temp->next; // Move to the next bogie
    }
    cout << endl; // New line after printing the list
}

// Function to delete the entire list
TrainBogie *deleteEntireList(TrainBogie *head)
{
    TrainBogie *temp = head; // Temporary pointer to traverse the list
    while (temp->next)
    { // Traverse to the last bogie
        TrainBogie*toDel = temp;

        temp = temp->next;
        delete toDel;
        
    }
}

27/10/2024, 22:49 - Vihaan: #include "myhome.h"
#include <iostream>

using namespace std;

// Constructor for Device class
Device::Device(const string &name, const string &id) : deviceName(name), deviceID(id), status(false) {}

// Turns the device on
void Device::turnOn() {
    status = true;
}

// Turns the device off
void Device::turnOff() {
    status = false;
}

// Returns the device ID
string Device::getDeviceID() const {
    return deviceID;
}

// Returns the device name
string Device::getDeviceName() const {
    return deviceName;
}

// Returns the device status
bool Device::getStatus() const {
    return status;
}

// Prints device information
void Device::printDeviceInfo() const {
    cout << deviceName << endl;
    cout << deviceID << endl;
    cout << (status ? "O" : "F") << endl;
}

// Adds a new device to the smart home
void SmartHome::addDevice(const string &name, const string &id) {
    Device newDevice(name, id);
    devices.push_back(newDevice);
}

// Controls the status of a specific device
int SmartHome::controlDevice(const string &deviceID) {
    for (auto &device : devices) {
        if (device.getDeviceID() == deviceID) {
            if (device.getStatus()) {
                device.turnOff();
            } else {
                device.turnOn();
            }
            return 1;
        }
    }
    return -1;
}

// Lists all devices and their statuses
void SmartHome::listDevices() const {
    for (const auto &device : devices) {
        device.printDeviceInfo();
    }
}

// Lists only the devices that are turned on
void SmartHome::listTurnedOnDevices() const {
    for (const auto &device : devices) {
        if (device.getStatus()) {
            device.printDeviceInfo();
        }
    }
}

\section*{Abstract}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Abstract}
This report describes our fine-grained bird classification pipeline using CUB-200-2011. We leverage a parameter-efficient EfficientNet-B0 backbone (~5.3M params), bounding-box cropping, and advanced augmentations. Training employs AdamW with label smoothing and cosine RL scheduling, achieving \textbf{83.07\%} test accuracy in 43 minutes.

\section*{Introduction}

Fine-grained image classification is a specialized area within computer vision that focuses on distinguishing between highly similar sub-categories within a broader object class. Unlike general object recognition (e.g., classifying ``dog'' vs. ``cat''), fine-grained classification aims to differentiate between distinct species of dogs, types of cars, or, in the context of this project, specific bird species. This task presents unique challenges due to the subtle visual differences that often distinguish one sub-category from another, requiring models to learn minute details rather than broad features.

The ability to accurately perform fine-grained classification has significant implications across various domains. In biological research, it can aid in biodiversity monitoring, species identification, and ecological studies. For conservation efforts, precise identification of endangered species is critical. Beyond biology, applications extend to quality control in manufacturing, medical diagnosis, and even specialized security systems.

This project specifically tackles fine-grained bird classification using the \textbf{CUB-200-2011 (Caltech-UCSD Birds 200-2011)} dataset. This dataset was chosen because it is a widely recognized benchmark for fine-grained classification, featuring 200 distinct bird species with significant intra-class variations (e.g., different poses, lighting conditions, partial occlusions) and subtle inter-class differences (e.g., closely related species with only minor plumage variations). These characteristics make CUB-200-2011 an ideal testbed for evaluating the robustness and discriminative power of deep learning models in challenging scenarios.

The primary goals of this mini-project are threefold:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Develop an efficient and effective deep learning pipeline} for fine-grained bird classification using a modern convolutional neural network architecture.
    \item \textbf{Achieve competitive classification accuracy} on the CUB-200-2011 test set, demonstrating the model's ability to distinguish between highly similar bird species.
    \item \textbf{Optimize for parameter efficiency and training time}, showcasing a practical approach to fine-grained classification that balances performance with computational resources.
\end{enumerate}

This report details the comprehensive approach taken, from custom dataset handling and advanced data augmentation to model architecture selection and training methodology, culminating in an analysis of the achieved performance.



\section{Dataset and Preprocessing}
\subsection{Dataset Overview}
CUB-200-2011 includes 11,788 images across 200 species. We use the default train-test split, which consists of \textbf{5,994 images for training} and \textbf{5,794 images for testing}.

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={CUBDataset Implementation}, label={lst:cubdataset}]
import os
from PIL import Image
import torch
from torch.utils.data import Dataset

class CUBDataset(Dataset):
    def __init__(self, root_dir, split='train', transform=None):
        
        self.root_dir = root_dir
        self.transform = transform

        # 1) load image paths
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'images.txt')) as f:
            # {img_id: relative_path}
            self.id2path = {
                int(line.split()[0]): line.strip().split()[1]
                for line in f
            }

        # 2) load labels
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'image_class_labels.txt')) as f:
          self.id2label = {
              int(parts[0]): int(parts[1]) - 1
              for parts in (line.strip().split() for line in f)
          }

        # 3) load split flags
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'train_test_split.txt')) as f:
          self.id2split = {
              int(parts[0]): int(parts[1])
              for parts in (line.strip().split() for line in f)
          }

        # 4) load bounding boxes
        with open(os.path.join(root_dir, 'bounding_boxes.txt')) as f:
          self.id2bbox = {
              int(parts[0]): tuple(map(float, parts[1:]))
              for parts in (line.strip().split() for line in f)
          }

        # 5) filter by split
            ids = [
                i for i in self.id2path
                if self.id2split[i] == (1 if split=='train' else 0)
            ]
        self.samples = [
            (i, self.id2path[i], self.id2label[i], self.id2bbox[i])
            for i in ids
        ]

    def __len__(self):
        return len(self.samples)

    def __getitem__(self, idx):
        img_id, rel_path, label, bbox = self.samples[idx]
        img_path = os.path.join(self.root_dir, 'images', rel_path)
        img = Image.open(img_path).convert('RGB')

        # --- CROP to bounding box ---
        x, y, w, h = bbox
        left   = int(x)
        top    = int(y)
        right  = int(x + w)
        bottom = int(y + h)
        img = img.crop((left, top, right, bottom))

        # --- apply transforms ---
        if self.transform:
            img = self.transform(img)

        return img, label

\end{lstlisting}
\section{Custom Dataset Class: \textsf{CUBDataset}}

To efficiently handle the CUB-200-2011 dataset, we implemented a custom PyTorch \textsf{Dataset} class named \textsf{CUBDataset}. This class enables organized loading of images along with bounding boxes and labels, while supporting transformations during training and testing.

\subsection*{Key Components}

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Initialization (\textsf{\_\_init\_\_})}: Loads metadata from the dataset directory including:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textsf{images.txt}: Maps image IDs to relative paths.
        \item \textsf{image\_class\_labels.txt}: Maps image IDs to class labels (0-indexed).
        \item \textsf{train\_test\_split.txt}: Defines whether an image is part of training or test set.
        \item \textsf{bounding\_boxes.txt}: Stores bounding box coordinates (x, y, width, height).
    \end{itemize}
    
    \item \textbf{Split Handling}: Based on the `split` argument ('train' or 'test'), the dataset filters and stores only the relevant images and their metadata.

    \item \textbf{\textsf{\_\_getitem\_\_} Method}:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Loads the image using PIL.
        \item Crops the image to its bounding box using the provided coordinates.
        \item Applies the provided transformation (e.g., augmentation, resizing).
        \item Returns the transformed image and its corresponding label.
    \end{itemize}

    \item \textbf{Bounding Box Cropping Logic}:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Bounding box coordinates \textsf{(x, y, width, height)} are converted to \textsf{(left, top, right, bottom)}.
        \item This ensures that only the bird (foreground) is used for training, improving model focus and reducing background noise.
    \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

% --- Bounding Box Cropping Visual Example ---

    \label{fig:bbox_cropping_example}

\subsection*{Why This Matters}

Using a custom dataset class gave us full control over the data pipeline. This allowed:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Seamless integration of bounding-box cropping.
    \item Consistent train/test splits as per official metadata.
    \item Flexibility to apply different transforms in training vs evaluation.
\end{itemize}


\section{Model Training Pipeline}

The training script (\textsf{train\_efficientnet.py}) implements the full pipeline for training EfficientNet-B0 on the CUB-200-2011 dataset. The code is modular, readable, and optimized for parameter efficiency and accuracy.
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
import os, time
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.optim as optim
from torch.optim.lr_scheduler import CosineAnnealingWarmRestarts
from torch.utils.data import DataLoader
import torchvision.transforms as T
from torchvision.models import efficientnet_b0, EfficientNet_B0_Weights
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

from data_loader import CUBDataset 

# 1. Config
DEVICE      = torch.device("cuda" if torch.cuda.is_available() else "cpu")
DATA_ROOT   = "CUB_200_2011/CUB_200_2011"
BATCH_SIZE  = 64
NUM_EPOCHS  = 40
LR          = 1e-4
WEIGHT_DEC  = 1e-4
NUM_CLASSES = 200
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{1. Configuration and Hyperparameters}
We first configure essential parameters:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textsf{DEVICE}: Use GPU if available.
  \item \textsf{BATCH\_SIZE}: 64
  \item \textsf{NUM\_EPOCHS}: 40
  \item \textsf{LR}: 1e-4 (learning rate), \textsf{WEIGHT\_DECAY}: 1e-4
  \item \textsf{NUM\_CLASSES}: 200 (as per the dataset)
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
train_transform = T.Compose([
    T.Resize((256, 256)),
    T.RandomCrop(224),
    T.RandomHorizontalFlip(),
    T.AutoAugment(policy=T.AutoAugmentPolicy.IMAGENET),
    T.ColorJitter(brightness=0.2, contrast=0.2),
    T.ToTensor(),
    T.RandomErasing(p=0.5),
])
test_transform = T.Compose([
    T.Resize((224, 224)),
    T.ToTensor(),
])
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection{Data Augmentation and Preprocessing}
Transformations are applied using \texttt{torchvision.transforms}. Data augmentation is crucial in deep learning, especially for fine-grained classification, as it helps to increase the diversity of the training data, thereby improving model generalization and reducing overfitting.

\textbf{Training transforms:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Resize to $256\times256$, then randomly crop to $224\times224$
    \item Random horizontal flip
    \item \textbf{AutoAugment (ImageNet policy)}: This technique automatically searches for an optimal data augmentation policy from a predefined set of operations. It helps to find a combination of transformations that significantly improves model accuracy and robustness, often outperforming hand-designed policies.
    \item Color jitter (brightness and contrast)
    \item \textbf{Random Erasing ($p=0.5$)}: This augmentation randomly selects a rectangular region in an image and erases its pixels with a random value. It acts as a form of regularization, making the model more robust to occlusions and preventing it from relying too heavily on specific features, thereby enhancing its generalization capabilities.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Testing transforms:}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Resize to $224\times224$ and convert to tensor
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
train_ds = CUBDataset(DATA_ROOT, split='train', 
transform=train_transform)
test_ds  = CUBDataset(DATA_ROOT, split='test',  transform=test_transform)
train_loader = DataLoader(train_ds, batch_size=BATCH_SIZE, shuffle=True,  num_workers=4)
test_loader  = DataLoader(test_ds,  batch_size=BATCH_SIZE, shuffle=False, num_workers=4)
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{3. Dataset and DataLoader}
We use the custom \textsf{CUBDataset} class with bounding box cropping enabled. Dataloaders are created separately for training and testing with appropriate shuffling and parallelization.
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
weights = EfficientNet_B0_Weights.IMAGENET1K_V1
model = efficientnet_b0(weights=weights)
model.classifier[1] = nn.Linear(model.classifier[1].in_features, 
NUM_CLASSES)
model = model.to(DEVICE)

\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{Model Initialization}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Backbone}: We utilize a pretrained EfficientNet-B0 model from \texttt{torchvision.models}, initialized with weights from the ImageNet dataset. This leverages transfer learning, allowing the model to benefit from features learned on a large, diverse dataset.
    \item \textbf{Classifier Modification}: The original classification head of the EfficientNet-B0 model is replaced with a new fully connected (FC) layer. This new layer's input features match the output features of the EfficientNet backbone, and its output dimension is set to \texttt{NUM\_CLASSES} (200, corresponding to the bird species in CUB-200-2011). \textit{During initial training phases, the backbone layers were kept frozen to allow the new classifier head to adapt quickly to the specific task; subsequently, the entire model was fine-tuned.}
    \item \textbf{Device Transfer}: The entire model is then transferred to the selected device (CPU/GPU) for computation.
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
criterion = nn.CrossEntropyLoss(label_smoothing=0.1)
optimizer = optim.AdamW(model.parameters(), lr=LR, 
weight_decay=WEIGHT_DEC)
scheduler = CosineAnnealingWarmRestarts(optimizer, T_0=10, 
T_mult=2, eta_min=1e-5)
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{Loss, Optimizer, and Scheduler}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Loss}: CrossEntropyLoss with label smoothing $=0.1$.
    \item \textbf{Optimizer}: AdamW with weight decay.
    \item \textbf{Scheduler}: Cosine Annealing WarmRestarts to periodically reset learning rate.
\end{itemize}
\begin{lstlisting}[caption={EfficientNet-B0 Training Script}, label={lst:efficientnet}]
train_losses, test_losses = [], []
train_accs, test_accs = [], []
best_acc = 0.0

start_time = time.time()

for epoch in range(1, NUM_EPOCHS+1):
    # ---- TRAIN ----
    model.train()
    total_loss, correct, total = 0.0, 0, 0
    for imgs, labels in train_loader:
        imgs, labels = imgs.to(DEVICE), labels.to(DEVICE)
        optimizer.zero_grad()
        outputs = model(imgs)
        loss = criterion(outputs, labels)
        loss.backward()
        optimizer.step()
        total_loss += loss.item() * imgs.size(0)
        preds = outputs.argmax(1)
        correct += preds.eq(labels).sum().item()
        total += labels.size(0)
    train_loss = total_loss / total
    train_acc = 100. * correct / total

    # ---- EVAL ----
    model.eval()
    total_loss, correct, total = 0.0, 0, 0
    with torch.no_grad():
        for imgs, labels in test_loader:
            imgs, labels = imgs.to(DEVICE), labels.to(DEVICE)
            outputs = model(imgs)
            loss = criterion(outputs, labels)
            total_loss += loss.item() * imgs.size(0)
            preds = outputs.argmax(1)
            correct += preds.eq(labels).sum().item()
            total += labels.size(0)
    test_loss = total_loss / total
    test_acc = 100. * correct / total

    scheduler.step()

    train_losses.append(train_loss)
    train_accs.append(train_acc)
    test_losses.append(test_loss)
    test_accs.append(test_acc)

    print(f"Epoch {epoch:02d}/{NUM_EPOCHS}  "
          f"Train: loss={train_loss:.4f}, acc={train_acc:.2f}%  "
          f"Test:  loss={test_loss:.4f}, acc={test_acc:.2f}%")

    # Save best checkpoint
    if test_acc > best_acc:
        best_acc = test_acc
        os.makedirs("models", exist_ok=True)
        torch.save(model.state_dict(), "models/best_efficientnet_b0.pth")
\end{lstlisting}
\subsection*{6. Training and Evaluation Loop}
The model is trained over 40 epochs. In each epoch:
\begin{itemize}
  \item The model is trained and evaluated.
  \item Loss and accuracy are computed for both splits.
  \item If the test accuracy improves, the model checkpoint is saved.
  \item The scheduler updates the learning rate using cosine annealing.
\end{itemize}

Performance is printed for each epoch, showing:
\textsf{Epoch X: Train loss/acc — Test loss/acc}

\subsection*{7. Final Outputs}
After training:
\begin{itemize}
  \item The best checkpoint is saved to \textsf{models/best\_efficientnet\_b0.pth}.
  \item Plots of loss and accuracy curves are saved as:
    \begin{itemize}
      \item \textsf{loss\_curve\_efficientnet.png}
      \item \textsf{accuracy\_curve\_efficientnet.png}
    \end{itemize}
  \item Total training time is printed.
\end{itemize}


\section{Model Architecture}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Backbone: EfficientNet-B0 (pretrained ImageNet)
    \item Parameters: ~5.3M
    \item Classifier: Final FC to 200 outputs
\end{itemize}

\section{Training Details}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Loss: Cross-Entropy with label smoothing (0.1)
    \item Optimizer: AdamW (lr=1e-4, wd=1e-4)
    \item Scheduler: CosineAnnealingWarmRestarts ($T_0$=10, $T_{mult}$=2, $\eta_{min}$=1e-5)
    \item Batch size: 64; Epochs: 40; Device: NVIDIA T4
\end{itemize}

\section{Results and Analysis}
\subsection{Performance Metrics}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Top-1 Test Accuracy: \textbf{83.07\%}
    \item Training Time: \textbf{43 min}
    \item Model Size: \textbf{~16.6 MB}
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Training Curves}
\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{loss_curve_efficientnet.png}
    \caption{Loss vs. Epoch}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.75\textwidth]{accuracy_curve_efficientnet.png}
    \caption{Accuracy vs. Epoch}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Observations}
\begin{itemize}
    \item Bounding box cropping significantly improved model focus and accuracy by isolating the primary subject (bird) and reducing background noise.
    \item Label smoothing and AutoAugment provided strong regularization, preventing overfitting and enhancing the model's ability to generalize to unseen data by introducing diverse variations during training.
    \item Cosine Annealing WarmRestarts scheduling stabilized late-epoch training by periodically resetting the learning rate, allowing the optimizer to escape local minima and continue exploring the loss landscape for better solutions.
    \item \textbf{Analysis of Curves}: As observed in Figure 3, the training and test loss curves exhibit a rapid decrease in the initial epochs, indicating efficient learning. Both curves converge steadily, showing good model stability and a lack of significant overfitting, as the test loss closely tracks the training loss. Similarly, Figure 4 demonstrates a steep increase in both training and test accuracy during the early phases of training, followed by a plateau. This indicates that the model quickly learned the primary features and then gradually refined its understanding of the more subtle, fine-grained distinctions. The close proximity of the train and test accuracy curves suggests good generalization performance.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Comparison with Related Work}
The achieved Top-1 Test Accuracy of 83.07\% is competitive for an EfficientNet-B0 backbone on the challenging CUB-200-2011 dataset. While state-of-the-art results on this dataset often involve larger models or more complex methodologies (e.g., attention mechanisms, knowledge distillation), our approach demonstrates a strong balance between performance and the inherent parameter efficiency of the EfficientNet-B0 architecture. This result highlights the effectiveness of the chosen preprocessing steps and training configuration for fine-grained tasks.

\subsection{Error Analysis }
To further understand the model's limitations and guide future improvements, a detailed error analysis would be beneficial. This would involve examining misclassified images to identify common patterns. For instance, misclassifications might occur between visually similar species (e.g., different types of sparrows or warblers), due to unusual bird poses, poor image quality, or challenging lighting conditions. Such analysis could inform targeted data augmentation strategies or architectural modifications to address specific failure modes.

\section{Conclusion}
Our EfficientNet-B0 approach balances performance and parameter efficiency, reaching competitive accuracy. Future work: larger EfficientNet variants, semi-supervised methods, ensembling.

13/08/2025, 23:39 - Parth IITB: In class i misunderstood the p-value to be  The p-value is the probability that the null hypothesis is true i.e A p-value of 0.05 does not mean there is a 5% chance the null hypothesis is correct but after a bit of researching on p-value I found
What a P-Value Is: A Measure of Surprise
At its core, a p-value is a measure of incompatibility between your observed data and a specific statistical model, known as the null hypothesis (H_0). The null hypothesis is a default stance, typically stating that there is no effect, no difference, or no relationship.
Imagine you are testing a new drug to lower blood pressure. The null hypothesis would be that the drug has no effect on blood pressure. You then collect data from a clinical trial.
The p-value answers a very specific question:
If the null hypothesis were true (i.e., the drug is completely useless), what is the probability of observing data that is at least as extreme as what you actually observed?
A small p-value (e.g., 0.03) means that your observed result would be very surprising, it would only happen 3% of the time—if the drug truly had no effect. This "surprise" leads you to doubt the null hypothesis and consider the alternative.
Conversely, a large p-value (e.g., 0.40) indicates that your data is quite compatible with the null hypothesis.

20/08/2025, 22:55 - Parth IITB: Omnibus test as suggested by the name, It's a global test that looks at the overall picture, testing a general hypothesis that involves multiple parameters simultaneously.
For example:
To determine if there was a difference in final exam scores based on three different teaching methods (e.g., 'Standard Lecture,' 'Group Project,' and 'Online Hybrid'), a ANOVA was conducted.
It won’t tell what is the relationship between the modes of teaching, it will just give an answer to whether the final exam scores are related to the mode of teaching. 
It can be thought of as gatekeeper, it tell us whether something interesting is going on in our data or not.
Formalizing the typical hypothesis structure for Omnibus test:
Null Hypothesis (H0): All the regression coefficients are zero.
Alternative Hypothesis (Ha): At least one regression coefficient is not zero.

One Example of Omnibus test is:
F-test in ANOVA:
Suppose we want to answer our original question of is there any relationship between final scores and teaching method.
Our Null hypothesis would be mean scores of all the teaching method is same.
Alternative Hypothesis: At least one teaching method results in a different mean test score.
If your F-test yields a significant p-value, you reject the null hypothesis. This tells you that not all teaching methods are created equal. However, it doesn't tell you if method 1 is better than method 2, or if method 3 is different from the others.

03/09/2025, 22:19 - Parth IITB: In machine learning, activation functions help models understand and learn patterns. Two of the most common ones are the sigmoid function and the softmax function. At first, they seem different: sigmoid is usually used when there are only two classes (binary classification), while softmax is used when there are many classes (multi-class classification). But in reality, sigmoid is just a simpler version of softmax.

The softmax function takes a list of numbers (one for each possible class) and turns them into probabilities. The nice thing about softmax is that the probabilities are always between 0 and 1, and they always add up to 1, which makes them easy to interpret.

The sigmoid function, on the other hand, only takes one number and maps it to a value between 0 and 1. That’s why it’s so commonly used in binary classification: it directly represents the probability of belonging to the “positive” class.

Here’s the connection: in a two-class situation, the softmax formula naturally simplifies down to the sigmoid formula. In other words, if you use softmax for just two classes, it behaves exactly like sigmoid.

So the key idea is that sigmoid isn’t a totally different function—it’s simply the softmax function applied to the special case where there are only two possible classes.

07/09/2025, 23:24 - Vihaan: Decision trees split data into smaller groups by repeatedly cutting at certain points, with the aim of making the resulting groups more pure. The rule that decides where to split is based on an optimization criterion.

In classification tasks, the criterion measures impurity, which is how mixed the classes are in a node. The two most common ones are Gini impurity and entropy. Gini is calculated by summing the probability of each item being chosen times the probability of it being misclassified. Entropy, from information theory, measures the randomness of the data and is calculated as the negative sum of the probability of each class times the log of that probability. The tree tries to maximize information gain, which is the reduction in entropy after a split, so the new nodes are more uniform.

For regression, the usual criterion is Mean Squared Error (MSE). The tree looks for the split that minimizes the average squared difference between the actual values and the predicted mean of each node. This way, the data points in each group are closer to their local mean, and the overall prediction error is reduced.
